The Story of World Literature (1) (7) (4) by John Macy, Translated by Hu Zhongchi 210

Chapter 46: Scandinavian Literature.

Among the prose writers of the latter half of the nineteenth century, there are several who are extremely outstanding. Jens Peter Jacobsen is the greatest prose artist produced by Denmark. He was a patient who died young and wrote with poignant care, like Flaubert. He sought the right words, the right rhythm, and spent a great deal of time on it. The result was two novels, "Marie Grubbe" and "Niels Lyhne," which had never been seen in Denmark that beautiful, and a collection of short stories with the title Mingens. Although he wrote so little, his influence was great, and his completion set a standard for Danish and Norwegian writers who were dedicated to beauty, style, and art, and his influence extended beyond Denmark. He was translated into German and at least partially into English.

After Jacobsen's untimely death, the novelist who temporarily stood in a leading position was Herman Bang, a realist who made meticulous observations of the low-middle classes of his own people and towns. His best work is "Tiny People." There is also a naturalist novelist, Peter Nansen, whose best novel, "Short Cut," is a profound and powerful work. Edvard Brandes must also be mentioned, whose reputation is somewhat obscured by the fact that he is the brother of the great critic Georg Brandes. However, Edvard was a critic of great talent, a playwright, and the author of two outstanding novels. Later, there appeared a great novelist who, in terms of time and spirit, is considered our contemporary: Henrik Pontoppidan. Until he received the Nobel Prize in 1917, English and American readers had never heard of the limitations he placed on the great literature created by small countries. Here is another example. Pontoppidan is a novelist with the power and sympathy we see in the great writers of Russia. His most beautiful novel, Lykke-Per, although it tells of simple people without heroic color, is an epic poem in terms of its symmetry.

Presiding over these poets and novelists, guiding them forward and welcoming the best of them, is Georg Brandes, a critic who has been the most knowledgeable Catholic in Europe for more than half a century. His scope of knowledge is vast; he seems to have read everything written in Europe. He wrote studies of Shakespeare, Ibsen, Anatole France, and many others. His work for future generations, his philosophy of criticism, is "Main Currents in Nineteenth-Century Literature," which has now been completely translated into English in six volumes. It is the best possible introduction to the literature of the past one hundred and fifty years. Danish readers say that his style is seductive and fluent. I can well believe this, because the English translation is also clear and fluent. His countrymen, even when they disagree with him, pay him the highest respect. He is probably the most famous critic in Europe, including all countries. I have no better way than to quote from him a section summarizing the characteristics of Danish and German literature. Brandes was not Scandinavian, but a Jew. It is appropriate to remember that he stood in a slightly objective view, seeing the Danish people. In the second volume of "Main Currents," "The German Romantic School," he said,

"Danish writers, seen as a whole, can be said to have the value of avoiding the tasteless and indulgent fantasies that Germans have often committed. The Danish stop in time.

Scene from the play "Uncle Vanya" by the Xinyou Drama Society.

They avoid paradoxes or fail to bring them to their logical conclusions. They have a solidity based on a natural desire to maintain balance and a natural inclination to tranquility. They have almost never become arrogant, presumptuous, revolutionary, extremely fantastic, entirely sentimental, entirely untrue, or entirely sensual. They are not very reckless, they never rush into the clouds, and they never fall into the well. This is precisely what makes them extremely familiar to their own people, and what characterizes the best works of Oehlenschläger and Hartmann... Unfailing taste and elegance will always be praised by the Danish as an expression of noble and self-controlled art... Think of Hoffman and his disciple Hans Christian Andersen, and then observe how much more healthy, calm, and gentle Andersen is than his teacher."

Norwegian literature and Danish literature are difficult to separate. After the political separation in the early nineteenth century, a movement arose to build an independent Norwegian literature. Many great figures of Danish literature were Norwegian. Some overly patriotic Norwegians tried hard to pull those writers back, but this is as futile as trying to decide whether Henry James and John Singer Sargent are American.

A later generation of romantic poets, yet all visionary poets must still be considered romantic, is Knut Hamsun. He was born in the mid-nineteenth century and lived into the twentieth. He began his adult life as a marine painter, then abandoned his paintbrush and took up the pen to describe the sea with words. He can be said to be the Danish John Masefield. His protagonists are sailors and fishermen. He became a healthy kind of patriot and became the head of the Danish theater scene by praising the life of the nation in strong poetic dramas. He made long voyages to study the appearance of the sea and the people who sailed it, thus making his content fresh, active, and true. However, knowing the sea all one's life does not necessarily mean being able to express the sea. Only those who have poetic genius not outside the ocean but inside themselves can become Conrad and Knut Hamsun.
